Sixth Objection: 'Did We Not Lift Your Burden'
Al-Iji presents the sixth objection and gives three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection regarding the Prophet's burden and the three answers Al-Iji provides.

Core Idea
The word 'wizr' can mean burden, not just sin.

This lesson examines a key objection drawn from the Qur'anic verse 'Did We not lift your burden?' Critics argue that this verse implies the Prophet had sins. Al-Iji responds with three distinct interpretations that preserve prophetic infallibility while respecting the verse's wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection verse
Start by reading the Arabic of the objection: 'ووضعنا عنك وزرك الذي أنقض ظهرك'. Understand that 'wizr' is the key word.
The objection hinges on the meaning of 'wizr' as sin.
2
Examine the three answers
Identify the three answers Al-Iji gives: (1) sins before prophethood, (2) leaving the better, (3) metaphorical heaviness.
Each answer offers a different way to reconcile the verse with prophetic sinlessness.
3
Understand the third answer deeply
Note how the third answer interprets 'wizr' as heaviness, supported by other Qur'anic verses. This is the most comprehensive answer.
The surrounding verses in Surah Sharh reinforce this interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( السادس : ووضعنا عنك وزرك الذي أنقض ظهرك ) والوزر هو الذنب وإنقاضه الظهر يدل على كبره ( الجواب : ( بأن الوزر المذكور محمول على ما كان قد اقترفه ( قبل النبوة أو ( هو ) ترك الأولى ) والإنقاض حينئذ محمول على استعظامه إياه ، ( أو ) نقول إنه قد جاء بمعنى الثقل كقوله تعالى : حتى تضع الحرب أوزارها ﴾ [محمد : ٤] ، فجاز أن يكون هاهنا مستعملا للثقل الذي كان عليه من الغم الشديد ( لإصرار قومه ) على إنكاره والشرك بالله ولعدم استطاعته على تنفيذ أمر الدين فلما أعلى الله شأنه وشد أزره فقد وضع عنه وزره وثقله ويقوي هذا التأويل قوله تعالى : ورفعنا لك ذكرك [ الشرح : ٤ ]، وقوله : إن مع ) العسر يسراً ﴾ [الشرح : ٦]
The sixth: 'And did We not lift from you your burden which weighed down your back?' And al-wizr is sin, and its weighing down the back indicates its greatness. The answer: That the mentioned burden is interpreted as what he had committed before prophethood, or it is leaving what is better. And the weighing down is then interpreted as his deeming it great. Or we say that it came in the sense of heaviness, as His saying: 'Until the war lays down its burdens' [Muhammad 4], so it is possible that it is used here for the heaviness that was upon him from severe grief due to his people's persistence in denying him and associating partners with Allah, and his inability to execute the affair of religion. When Allah elevated his status and strengthened his support, he lifted from him his burden and heaviness. And this interpretation is strengthened by His saying: 'And we raised for you your remembrance' [Sharh 4] and His saying: 'Indeed, with hardship comes ease' [Sharh 6].
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The objection cites the verse 'Did We not lift your burden' claiming it indicates sin. Al-Iji provides three answers. First, the burden refers to minor sins before prophethood. Second, it refers to leaving the better (tark al-awla). Third, 'burden' (wizr) is used metaphorically for heaviness, as in the Qur'anic phrase 'lay down its burdens', meaning the grief and distress caused by his people's rejection. This interpretation is supported by the surrounding verses about raising his remembrance and ease after hardship.

ذنب
ترك الأولى
نبوة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'wizr' in the verse necessarily means sin.
Correction: The word 'wizr' can also mean burden or heaviness, as in the Qur'anic phrase 'lay down its burdens' (Muhammad 4). The context of Surah Sharh supports a metaphorical interpretation of burden as grief and difficulty.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Seventh Objection: Forgiveness Verses and Their Answers
Al-Iji presents the seventh objection using forgiveness verses and gives three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconcile verses that speak of the Prophet's forgiveness with the doctrine of his sinlessness.

Core Idea
The Prophet's 'forgiven sins' are not real sins but pre-prophetic lapses or metaphorical attribution.

The seventh objection in the mawaqif uses Quranic verses that seem to attribute sins to the Prophet. Al-Iji presents three answers that protect the doctrine of 'isma by reinterpreting the verses as referring to pre-prophetic actions, leaving the better, or attributing the sins of the community to their leader.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and verses
Read the three Quranic verses presented in the objection. Notice how they mention forgiveness and repentance.
2
Understand the apparent implication
The objection claims that repentance implies sin, so the Prophet must have sinned. Note that this contradicts sinlessness.
3
Study the first answer: before prophethood
Al-Iji says the verses refer to minor sins before prophethood. 'First and last' means before and after prophethood but without textual indication.
4
Study the second answer: leaving the better
'Sin' is actually leaving the better (tark al-awla), called 'sin' hyperbolically.
5
Study the third answer: attribution from the community
The sins are attributed to the Prophet as leader; the forgiveness is for his nation. Also note the weak grammatical interpretation that is rejected.

(السابع قوله : ليغفر لك الله ما تقدم من ذنبك وما تأخر [الفتح : ٢]، و قوله : ( واستغفر لذنبك ﴾ [غافر: ٥٥]، و) قوله : ( لقد ( تاب الله على النبي ﴾ [التوبة : ١١٧ ] ) إذ لا وجود للتوبة إلا مع الذنب، ( الجواب أنه قبل النبوة وحمله على ما تقدم النبوة وما تأخر عنها لادلالة للفظ عليه ) ، إذ يجوز أن يصدر عنه قبل النبوة صغيرتان إحداهما متقدمة على الأخرى ( أو ) أنه ( ترك الأولى ) وتسميته بالذنب استعظام لصدوره عنه ( أو ) نقول : (نسب إليه ذنب قومه ) فإن رئيس القوم قد ينسب إليه ما فعله بعض أتباعه فالمعنى ليغفر لأجلك ما تقدم من ذنب أمتك، وما تأخر منه واستغفر لذنب أمتك وتاب الله على أمة النبيله وأتباعه ( وأما ما يقال أن المصدر مضاف إلى المفعول فالمعنى ذنب قومك إليك ) أي ما ارتكبوه من الذنوب بالنسبة إليك كأنواع إيذائهم إياك فلا يخفى ضعفه فإن ذلك ) إنما يتأتى ( في المصادر المتعدية)، والذنب ليس منها والاكتفاء بأدنى تعلق إضافة الذنب إليه مما لا يقبله ذوق سليم .
The seventh: His saying: 'That Allah may forgive you your sin, the first and the last' [al-Fath 2], and His saying: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin' [Ghafir 55], and His saying: 'Allah has certainly forgiven the Prophet' [al-Tawba 117] — since repentance does not occur except with sin. The answer: That it was before prophethood, and interpreting the 'first and last' as before and after prophethood has no indication from the wording. It is possible that two minor sins occurred from him before prophethood, one preceding the other. Or that it is leaving what is better, and calling it sin is deeming it great. Or we say: The sin of his people is attributed to him, for the leader of a people may be attributed what some of his followers did. So the meaning is: that for your sake He may forgive what preceded of the sin of your nation and what followed, and ask forgiveness for the sin of your nation, and Allah has forgiven the nation of the Prophet and his followers. And as for what is said that the verbal noun is annexed to the object, so the meaning is 'the sin of your people towards you' — that is, what they committed of sins regarding you, such as types of their harming you. Its weakness is not hidden, for that applies only to transitive verbal nouns, and 'sin' is not among them. Contenting oneself with the least attachment of the annexation of sin to him is not accepted by a sound taste.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection presents three verses: one promising forgiveness of 'sins' (al-Fath 2), one commanding the Prophet to ask forgiveness (Ghafir 55), and one stating Allah repented to the Prophet (al-Tawba 117). These seem to imply he committed sins. Al-Iji gives three answers. First, these refer to minor sins before prophethood. Second, 'sin' here means leaving the better (tark al-awla), and the term is used hyperbolically. Third, the 'sins' are attributed to the Prophet as the leader of his community; the forgiveness is for the sins of his nation. A weak alternative interpretation that the annexation is of the object (i.e., sins committed against him) is rejected as grammatically unsound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The forgiveness verses prove that the Prophet committed real sins after prophethood.
Correction: Al-Iji explains that the verses refer either to minor sins before prophethood, to leaving the better (not real sin), or to sins attributed from the community.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the answers Al-Iji gives for the seventh objection?
A) The verses refer to sins before prophethood.
B) The verses refer to leaving the better (tark al-awla).
C) The verses refer to sins imputed from the community.
D) The verses refer to major sins after prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji defends the Prophet's sinlessness by reinterpreting forgiveness verses as referring to pre-prophetic minor lapses, leaving the better, or the sins of the community attributed to their leader.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'That Allah may forgive you your sin,' do you feel a secret relief that even the Prophet needed forgiveness, and thus your own sins are less serious? How does Al-Iji's interpretation challenge that comfort, and what does your reaction reveal about your heart's estimation of the Prophet's rank?
?
If you were in the Prophet's place and someone attributed your community's sins to you, would you accept that as a leader or feel unfairly burdened? What does that tell you about the level of responsibility you avoid in your own life?
?
You have likely used the word 'sin' loosely for minor faults. After this lesson, will you ever again call a mere 'leaving the better' a sin? How might that change the way you speak about your own shortcomings and those of others?
